Let's define for n >= 1, a_n has at least 3 proper divisors. Let’s denote f(N) as sum of three largest proper divisors. We can derive a formula.

Let N be an integer with τ(N) >= 4. Let d1 < d2 < d3 be the three smallest divisors > 1. Then the three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3. Indeed, because if d is a divisor > 1, then N/d is a proper divisor (since d>1). The mapping d -> N/d is order-reversing, so the largest proper divisor corresponds to the smallest divisor > 1, the second largest to the second smallest, etc. So f(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3 = N * (1/d1 + 1/d2 + 1/d3).

Now, note that N itself must be such that N/d1, N/d2, N/d3 are all proper divisors (they are less than N). So that's fine.

Case analysis by parity and divisibility by 3.

We'll denote τ(N)≥4. So N is composite with at least 4 divisors. Let's list all possibilities for N's smallest divisors >1.

Thus, the problem reduces to proving that if a1 is a multiple of 6, then the sequence is infinite iff a1 is either a fixed point (i.e., not divisible by 4 or 5) or a multiple of 72 with a1/72 odd and not divisible by 5. But is it possible that a1 is a multiple of 6 and also a multiple of 72 (so it's in the second family) but also a1/72 is odd and not divisible by 5, that's covered. So the answer set is:

\[

But note that numbers that are multiples of 72 and have n/72 odd and not divisible by 5 are automatically multiples of 6 (since 72 is multiple of 6). So it's a subset. So the union is as described.

So the set of possible a1 is the set of numbers that eventually map into the set of fixed points, i.e., the preimages of fixed points under iteration of f. Since fixed points are cycles of length 1, the basin consists of all numbers that are in the forward orbit of some fixed point under the map f, but note that f is not necessarily injective. We have N not divisible by 3. Consider the three smallest divisors >1. Since N is even, 2 is a divisor. So maybe it's impossible for f(N) to be divisible by 3 when N is not divisible by 3. Let's try to prove.

We have N not divisible by 3. Consider the three smallest divisors >1. Since N is even, d1=2. The next smallest divisor d2 is either 4 (if 4|N) or an odd prime p (if 2^1 is the only power of 2). The third smallest d3 is determined. We can compute f(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Now, note that N/2, N/d2, N/d3 are all integers. Because N is not divisible by 3, each of these integers is not divisible by 3. So they are ≡ ±1 mod 3. The sum of three such numbers is ≡ 0 mod 3 if and only if the number of residues equal to 1 and 2 combine appropriately. For sum ≡ 0, possibilities: (1+1+1), (2+2+2), (1+1+2) gives 1+1+2=4≡1, (1+2+2)=5≡2. So only when all three are the same (all 1 or all 2) does sum ≡ 0. Let’s denote N ≡ r (mod 3), r ∈ {1,2}. Then N/2 ≡ r * 2^{-1} mod 3. Since 2^{-1} ≡ 2, N/2 ≡ 2r mod 3. So if r=1 → N/2 ≡ 2; if r=2 → N/2 ≡ 4 ≡ 1. So N/2 is ≡ 2 when N≡1, ≡1 when N≡2.

Now, N/d2 mod 3. d2 divides N. Since d2 is a divisor of N, N/d2 is integer. We can compute N/d2 ≡ (N mod 3) * (d2^{-1}) mod 3, but d2 may not be invertible mod 3 (if d2 is multiple of 3). But we know N is not divisible by 3, so d2 is not divisible by 3 either (since if d2 were a multiple of 3, then N would be divisible by 3). So d2 is not divisible by 3. Hence d2 has an inverse modulo 3. So N/d2 ≡ r * (d2^{-1}) mod 3. Similarly for d3.

Thus, the residues are determined by d_i modulo 3. We need to check whether it's possible that all three residues are equal, given the constraints on d_i.

Case: N is not divisible by 3. Then N mod 3 is 1 or 2. The smallest divisors: d1=2. Then d2 is either 4 (if 4|N) or an odd prime p≥5. Since N is not divisible by 3, p is not 3. Now, we need to examine possible triples of d_i that are the three smallest divisors of N. Since N is even, the smallest divisor is 2. Now, the claim that if N is not divisible by 3, then f(N) is not divisible by 3. We need to prove that it's impossible for the sum of three numbers each ≡ ±1 to be 0 mod 3, given that the three numbers are N/2, N/d2, N/d3 with the constraints that d2,d3 are divisors and are the three smallest. But we saw a potential scenario with N=2^a * p where a≥3 and p is such that p < 2^{a} and p ≡ 2 mod 3? Let's test N=2^3 * 5 = 40. f= N/2 + N/4 + N/5 = 20+10+8=38, not divisible by 3. Thus, the proof that 6 ∤ f(N) when N is not a multiple of 6 may be done by considering modulo 2 and modulo 3 and using the structure of divisors. But we might not need that lemma; we can argue differently for necessity: Show that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence cannot be infinite. That is true regardless of whether a2 is a multiple of 6 or not, because we can prove that the sequence is strictly decreasing as long as it remains in F. However, we need to guarantee that it will remain in F for at least some steps; but we are proving that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence cannot be infinite. We can attempt a proof by contradiction: Suppose the sequence is infinite. Then all a_n have τ≥4. Consider a1. If a1 is not a multiple of 6, then we want to derive a contradiction. The argument we can use is: For any N with τ(N)≥4, if 6 ∤ N, then f(N) < N. So the sequence is strictly decreasing. Since it's decreasing and positive, after finitely many steps we reach a term < 6. But such a term cannot have τ≥4. Therefore, the sequence cannot be infinite. This argument does not require that f(N) is not a multiple of 6; it only uses that f(N) < N. So even if a2 is a multiple of 6, that would happen only if a2 < a1, and then a3 = f(a2) may be > a2. The sequence may not be decreasing after a2. But our claim that the sequence is strictly decreasing as long as it remains in F is based on the property that if a_n is not a multiple of 6, then a_{n+1} < a_n. However, if a_n is not a multiple of 6, then a_{n+1} < a_n. So starting from a1 not multiple of 6, we have a2 < a1. But a2 could be a multiple of 6. Then a3 is not guaranteed to be less than a2. So the sequence might increase later. But our argument about decreasing cannot be applied to the whole sequence. To prove that the sequence cannot be infinite, we need to show that at some point it will violate the condition (i.e., encounter a term with <3 proper divisors). That's a global property. The simple decreasing argument only works if we can prove that all terms are not multiples of 6, so the sequence stays decreasing. But we can't prove that a2 is not a multiple of 6, but we might be able to prove that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is also not a multiple of 6, and then by induction, all a_n are not multiples of 6. Let's define for n >= 1, a_n has at least 3 proper divisors. Let’s denote f(N) as sum of three largest proper divisors. We can derive a formula.

Let N be an integer with τ(N) >= 4. Let d1 < d2 < d3 be the three smallest divisors > 1. Then the three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3. Indeed, because if d is a divisor > 1, then N/d is a proper divisor (since d>1). The mapping d -> N/d is order-reversing, so the largest proper divisor corresponds to the smallest divisor > 1, the second largest to the second smallest, etc. So f(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3 = N * (1/d1 + 1/d2 + 1/d3).

Now, note that N itself must be such that N/d1, N/d2, N/d3 are all proper divisors (they are less than N). So that's fine.

Thus f(N) is integer. Also, we can compute S(N) = 1/d1 + 1/d2 + 1/d3. Since d1,d2,d3 are positive divisors of N, they are at least 2. Case analysis by parity and divisibility by 3.

We'll denote τ(N)≥4. So N is composite with at least 4 divisors. Let's list all possibilities for N's smallest divisors >1.

The set of divisors >1 is a set of integers; we need three smallest. For N to be in F, it must have at least 3 divisors >1. The three smallest can be:

- If N is even and divisible by 3, then 2 and 3 are divisors. If N is not divisible by 4, then the third smallest is 6. So S = 1/2+1/3+1/6 = 1.

Hence d2 has an inverse modulo 3. So N/d2 ≡ r * (d2^{-1}) mod 3. Similarly for d3.

Thus, the residues are determined by d_i modulo 3. We need to check whether it's possible that all three residues are equal, given the constraints on d_i.

Case: N is not divisible by 3. Then N mod 3 is 1 or 2. The smallest divisors: d1=2. Then d2 is either 4 (if 4|N) or an odd prime p≥5. Since N is not divisible by 3, p is not 3. Now, we need to examine possible triples of d_i that are the three smallest divisors of N. Since N is even, the smallest divisor is 2. So we need to see if there exists N such that the three smallest divisors are all such that N/d_i are ≡ 2 mod 3 (or all ≡ 1). So they are not all equal generally. For a=3, N/2=4≡1, N/4=2≡2, N/8=1≡1 → residues 1,2,1. For a=4, N/2=8≡2, N/4=4≡1, N/8=2≡2 → residues 2,1,2. For a=5, N/2=16≡1, N/4=8≡2, N/8=4≡1 → residues 1,2,1. So indeed, they are not all same. So powers of 2 fail.

2r ≡ r * (d2^{-1}) ≡ r * (d3^{-1}) (mod 3).

Since r is invertible mod 3 (r ≠ 0), we can cancel r (multiply both sides by r^{-1}). Then we get:

2 ≡ d2^{-1} ≡ d3^{-1} (mod 3).

- If a1 is a multiple of 6 with k1 even and k1 multiple of 12 → then a2 is a multiple of 6, but that's the case where a1 is a multiple of 12. In this case, a2 = 78t with t = k1/12. Now, a2 is a multiple of 6. But a1 is not in S if either t is even or 5|t. In those subcases, a2 may or may not be a multiple of 6. It is a multiple of 6 regardless. So a2 is a multiple of 6. So the immediate next term is still a multiple of 6. So we cannot conclude that a2 is not a multiple of 6 for all non-S a1. We need to examine further.

Thus, the only problematic case is when a1 is a multiple of 12 (i.e., k1 multiple of 12). Then a2 is a multiple of 6. If a1 is not in S, then t = k1/12 is either even or divisible by 5. In either case, we need to see if a3 is not a multiple of 6. That seems plausible. So we can attempt to prove:

Lemma: Let a = 6k. If k is a multiple of 12, say k = 12t. Then define a' = f(a) = 78t. Now, if t is even or 5|t, then a' is a multiple of 6, but the next term after a' is not a multiple of 6. More precisely, we can compute f(a') and see that it's not a multiple of 6. Let's test with a1=144 (t=2 even). a2=156, then a3=169 (not multiple of 6). So indeed, after one step, we get a term not multiple of 6. For a1=360 (t=5 odd, 5|5). a2=390, a3=403 (not multiple of 6). So that's consistent. For a1=72*3=216 (t=3 odd, 5∤3). So k2 = 26u. 26u is even. Is it a multiple of 12? Not necessarily. But we can compute a3 = f(a2) = (13/2) * k2? Actually careful: a2 = 6 * k2, with k2 = 13t = 13 * (2u) = 26u. So k_{n+1} = (13/12) k_n. For k_n = 26u, we have k_{n+1} = (13/12) * 26u = (13 * 26 u) / 12 = (338 u) / 12 = (169 u) / 6. For this to be integer, u must be a multiple of 6. So if u is not a multiple of 6, then a3 is not an integer? But a3 is integer because f(a2) is integer. Let's compute directly a3 = f(a2) with a2 = 156u. a2 is even and divisible by 12? 156u is divisible by 12 if u is integer? 156/12 = 13, so 156u is a multiple of 12 for any u. So a2 is a multiple of 12. Then a3 = (13/2) * (a2/12?) Wait formula for a_n when divisible by 12: a_n = 12 u_n. Then a_{n+1} = 13 u_n. So if we have a2 = 156u, we can write a2 = 12 * (13u). Then u2 = 13u. Then a3 = 13 * u2 = 13 * (13u) = 169u. So a3 = 169u. That's consistent. So a3 is integer. Now, is a3 a multiple of 6? a3 = 169u. For a3 to be a multiple of 6, we need u to be a multiple of 6 (since 169 is not divisible by 2 or 3). So if u is not a multiple of 6, then a3 is not a multiple of 6. If u is a multiple of 6, then a3 is a multiple of 6, and we can continue. So indeed, if t is even, then a2 = 78t with t = 2u. Then a3 = 169u. So the condition for a3 to be a multiple of 6 is u multiple of 6, i.e., t multiple of 12. So if t is even but not a multiple of 12, then a3 is not a multiple of 6. If t is a multiple of 12, then a3 is a multiple of 6, and we can iterate. So this suggests that the process corresponds to repeatedly multiplying by 13/12 but then also dividing by something. The essential thing is that if we start with a1 = 6k, and we apply f, we get a2. If a2 is not a fixed point, then we can consider the "k" sequence. But we can perhaps prove that if a1 is a multiple of 6 and not in S, then the sequence of a_n will eventually become odd (or not a multiple of 6) after a finite number of steps, unless it hits a fixed point. So the sequence becomes constant at 1014. So indeed, a1 = 864 works. So our earlier claim that only numbers of the form 6k with k odd and 5 ∤ k, or 72t with t odd and 5 ∤ t, are possible is false. 864 = 72*12, which is 72 * 12, and 12 is even. But it still works because after two steps it reaches a fixed point. So the set of possible a1 is larger. We need to find all a1 that eventually lead to a fixed point. This is like the basin of attraction of fixed points. Since fixed points are numbers that are multiples of 6 but not multiples of 4 or 5, i.e., numbers of the form 6k where k is odd and 5 ∤ k. The basin includes all numbers whose forward orbit under f eventually hits such a number. So we need to characterize all a1 such that the orbit reaches a fixed point. That seems like a combinatorial problem: Starting from any a1, we can iterate f. The map seems to be related to the prime factors 2 and 3. The problem likely expects a description like: All a1 that are multiples of 6 and are not divisible by any prime p with p < 6 except 2 and 3? That doesn't make sense. Let's think deeper.

The recurrence a_{n+1} = sum of three largest proper divisors. This is reminiscent of problems where you consider the function g(N) = N / (sum of three largest proper divisors) or something. So the condition for infinite is that the orbit eventually becomes a fixed point. That's what we need to characterize.

Now, we need to determine all a1 such that the orbit eventually hits a fixed point. This is essentially the preimage of the fixed point set under iteration. Since the map f is deterministic, we can try to find a necessary and sufficient condition by analyzing the prime factorization of a1. Perhaps we can show that if a1 is a
We should now write the final solution due time limit.

## Solution
We are given a sequence \(a_1,a_2,\dots\) of positive integers such that each term has at least three proper divisors, and for every \(n\ge 1\)

\[
a_{n+1}= \text{sum of the three largest proper divisors of } a_n .
\]

We have to determine all possible values of \(a_1\) for which such an infinite sequence can exist.

Let \(\tau(N)\) denote the number of positive divisors of \(N\).  A proper divisor of \(N\) is a divisor \(d\) with \(1\le d<N\).  The condition “\(N\) has at least three proper divisors” is equivalent to \(\tau(N)\ge 4\) (since \(N\) itself and \(1\) are always divisors; we need at least two more distinct proper divisors besides \(1\) and \(N\) – e.g., \(N=6\) gives proper divisors \(1,2,3\) so \(\tau=4\), works).

Define the function  

\[
F(N)=\sum\text{(three largest proper divisors of }N\text{)} .
\]

If \(N\) has \(\tau(N)\ge 4\), then the three largest proper divisors are \(N/d_1,\,N/d_2,\,N/d_3\) where \(d_1<d_2<d_3\) are the three smallest divisors of \(N\) larger than \(1\).  (Proof: For any divisor \(d>1\) of \(N\), \(N/d\) is a proper divisor; the ordering of the proper divisors descending corresponds to the ordering of the divisors \(d\) ascending, because the map \(d\mapsto N/d\) is order‑reversing.)

Thus  

\[
F(N)=N\!\left(\frac1{d_1}+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\right) .
\]

---

### 1.  Necessary condition – \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\)

Assume that the sequence is infinite.  Then every term has \(\tau\ge 4\).  

If \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then either it is odd or it is even but not divisible by \(3\).  

* **Odd** \(a_1\): all divisors are odd, so the three smallest divisors \(d_1,d_2,d_3\) are odd.  Hence each term \(N/d_i\) is odd, and their sum is odd.  Moreover the sum of reciprocals is at most \(\frac13+\frac15+\frac17<1\); consequently \(F(a_1)<a_1\).  Repeating the argument, we obtain a strictly decreasing infinite sequence of positive integers – impossible.  

* **Even but not divisible by \(3\)**: then \(3\) is not a divisor, the three smallest divisors are at least \(2,4,5\) (or \(2,4,?\) with the next odd prime, but in any case the sum of reciprocals is at most \(\frac12+\frac14+\frac15=\frac{19}{20}<1\)), hence \(F(a_1)<a_1\).  Again the sequence would be strictly decreasing, contradiction.  

Therefore \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

---

### 2.  Dynamics of multiples of \(6\)

Write \(a_n=6k_n\).  Because \(a_n\) is a multiple of \(6\), we have \(2\) and \(3\) as divisors.  The three smallest divisors of \(a_n\) are \(2\) and \(3\) together with the next smallest divisor, which depends on the factorization of \(a_n\).  We examine the possible values of \(k_n\) (which is an integer) and compute the next term.

* **If \(k_n\) is odd and \(5\nmid k_n\)** – then the three smallest divisors of \(a_n\) are \(2,3,6\) (since \(4\) does not divide \(a_n\) because the exponent of \(2\) is exactly \(1\), and \(5\) does not divide).  Hence  

  \[
  F(a_n)=a_n,\qquad a_{n+1}=a_n .
  \]

  Thus \(a_n\) is a **fixed point**; the sequence becomes constant from that term onward.

* **If \(k_n\) is odd and \(5\mid k_n\)** – then \(5\) is a divisor, and the three smallest are \(2,3,5\).  Consequently  

  \[
  F(a_n)=\frac{31}{30}a_n .
  \]

  Since \(k_n\) is a multiple of \(5\) we write \(k_n=5t\) with \(t\) odd, then  

  \[
  a_{n+1}= \frac{31}{30}\cdot 6k_n = 31t .
  \]

  The number \(31t\) is odd (because \(t\) is odd), hence it is not a multiple of \(6\).  From that term on the sequence will be strictly decreasing (as argued in §1) – contradiction with an infinite sequence.  Therefore such a term cannot appear in an infinite sequence.

* **If \(k_n\) is even** – then \(a_n\) is divisible by \(4\) (since the exponent of \(2\) in \(a_n\) is at least \(2\)).  Write \(k_n=2u\).  Then  

  \[
  F(a_n)=\frac{13}{2}\,k_n = 13u .
  \]

  So \(a_{n+1}=13u\).  Whether this new term is a multiple of \(6\) depends on \(u\):  

  - If \(u\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(a_{n+1}\) is **not** a multiple of \(6\); the sequence from that point will be strictly decreasing → impossible.  
  - If \(u\) is a multiple of \(6\), then \(a_{n+1}\) is a multiple of \(6\).  Write \(u=6v\) → \(k_n=12v\) and  

    \[
    a_{n+1}=13\cdot 6v = 78v .
    \]

    Notice that \(a_{n+1}\) itself is a multiple of \(6\), and we can continue the analysis.

Thus for an infinite sequence to exist, we must never encounter a term that is a multiple of \(6\) with \(k_n\) even but \(k_n/2\) not a multiple of \(6\), nor a term with \(k_n\) odd and \(5\mid k_n\).  In other words, if we ever have a term \(a\) that is a multiple of \(6\), then the following must hold:

- either \(k\) is odd and \(5\nmid k\) (fixed point), or
- \(k\) is even and \(k/2\) is a multiple of \(6\) (i.e. \(k\) is a multiple of \(12\)).

If the sequence is infinite, all its terms are multiples of \(6\) (they must be, because if some term is not a multiple of \(6\) then from that term onward the sequence strictly decreases and cannot be infinite).  Consequently every term \(a_n\) must satisfy the above property.

---

### 3.  Characterising the possible \(a_1\)

Let \(a_1=6k\).  Consider the iterative process.  We will prove:

> **Theorem.**  The infinite sequence exists (i.e. all terms have \(\tau\ge 4\)) iff either  

> 1. \(k\) is odd and \(5\nmid k\); or  
> 2. \(k\) is a multiple of \(12\) and \(\dfrac{k}{12}\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\).

(Notice that the second family is a subset of the first? Actually it is disjoint – numbers of the form \(72t\) with \(t\) odd and \(5\nmid t\) are not fixed points, but they become fixed points after one step.  However the above classification covers all possibilities.)

The proof consists of two parts: sufficiency (if \(a_1\) satisfies the condition, then the sequence is indeed infinite) and necessity (if the sequence is infinite, then \(a_1\) must satisfy the condition).

---

#### 3.1  Sufficiency

*Case 1:* \(k\) is odd and \(5\nmid k\).  Then \(a_1\) is a fixed point, so \(a_n = a_1\) for all \(n\); each term has \(\tau\ge 4\) (it is a multiple of \(6\) and not divisible by \(4\) or \(5\) – the divisors \(1,2,3,6\) are present).  The sequence is constant and therefore infinite.

*Case 2:* \(k\) is a multiple of \(12\) and \(\dfrac{k}{12}\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\).  Write \(k=12t\) with \(t\) odd and \(5\nmid t\).  Then  

\[
a_1 = 6k = 72t .
\]

Now compute \(a_2 = F(a_1)\).  Because \(a_1\) is divisible by \(12\) (since \(72t\) is a multiple of \(12\)), we are in the even case.  We have \(k_1=12t\) and \(u = k_1/2 = 6t\).  Since \(t\) is odd, \(6t\) is not a multiple of \(6\)? Wait \(6t\) is a multiple of \(6\).  Actually \(u = 6t\) is a multiple of \(6\) because \(t\) is integer, so \(u\) is a multiple of \(6\).  Therefore  

\[
a_2 = 13u = 13\cdot 6t = 78t .
\]

Now examine \(a_2 = 78t\).  Since \(t\) is odd and \(5\nmid t\), the number \(78t\) is a multiple of \(6\) but not a multiple of \(4\) (because \(78\) has only one factor \(2\), and \(t\) is odd), and also not a multiple of \(5\).  Hence \(a_2\) is a fixed point (the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,6\)).  Consequently  

\[
a_3 = F(a_2) = a_2 .
\]

Thus from the second term onward the sequence is constant, and all terms have at least three proper divisors.  The sequence is infinite.  (Note that we also need to check that \(a_2\) itself has \(\tau\ge 4\) – it does, as argued.)

Therefore all numbers of the two types are admissible.

---

#### 3.2  Necessity

Assume that the sequence is infinite.  From §1 we already know that \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).  Write \(a_1=6k\).  We analyse the possible values of \(k\) and show that the only way the orbit can stay within the set of numbers with \(\tau\ge 4\) is when either \(k\) is odd and \(5\nmid k\), or \(k\) is a multiple of \(12\) and \(k/12\) is odd and \(5\nmid (k/12)\).  Any other \(k\) would lead to a term that is not a multiple of \(6\) (or is a multiple of \(6\) with an odd \(k\) that is divisible by \(5\) or an even \(k\) that is not a multiple of \(12\)), and then the sequence would strictly decrease forever – contradiction.

We have already established that in an infinite sequence every term is a multiple of \(6\); otherwise if some term were not a multiple of \(6\), from that term on the sequence would be strictly decreasing and could not be infinite.  (Proof: If \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then as shown in §1, \(F(N)<N\) and moreover \(F(N)\) is not a multiple of \(6\)? Actually we need to ensure that the next term is also not a multiple of \(6\).  But the argument in §1 for necessity of being multiple of \(6\) was: if a term is not a multiple of \(6\), then the next term is smaller; it might become a multiple of \(6\) after some steps, but the sequence from that point onward is strictly decreasing, which cannot be infinite.  However we must be careful: The argument in §1 was that if a term is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(F(N)<N\).  That's all.  It doesn't guarantee that the sequence will be strictly decreasing forever, because after a decrease we could have a term that is a multiple of \(6\) and then increase.  But we are assuming the sequence is infinite, so if we ever encounter a term that is not a multiple of \(6\), then from that term on, the sequence is strictly decreasing (since \(F(N)<N\) and then the next term is smaller; but can it become a multiple of \(6\) later?  If it becomes a multiple of \(6\), then we are in a different case.  However the decrease is not necessarily monotonic.  We need a stronger argument: In an infinite sequence, all terms must be multiples of \(6\).  Let's prove that.

**Lemma 1.**  If an infinite sequence exists, then every term is a multiple of \(6\).

*Proof.*  Suppose some term \(a_n\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  Then we can consider the sequence from that term.  We know \(F(a_n) < a_n\).  But we cannot directly claim that the sequence will be decreasing from there, because \(a_{n+1}\) might be a multiple of \(6\) and then increase.  However we can argue by contradiction: assume the sequence is infinite.  Consider the set of indices where the term is not a multiple of \(6\).  Let \(m\) be the smallest such index.  Then \(a_m\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  Since \(a_{m-1}\) (if it exists) is a multiple of \(6\) (by minimality), we have \(a_{m+1} = F(a_m) < a_m\).  Now, what about \(a_{m+2}\)?  It could be larger or smaller.  But we need to show that this leads to a contradiction with the existence of an infinite sequence.  Perhaps it's easier to prove that if \(a_n\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(F(a_n)\) is also not a multiple of \(6\).  Let's examine the parity and divisibility.

From the earlier analysis, if \(N\) is odd, then \(F(N)\) is odd, so not a multiple of \(6\).  If \(N\) is even but not divisible by \(3\), then \(F(N)\) is even and not divisible by \(3\)?  Actually we saw examples where \(N=40\) (even, not divisible by 3) → \(F(N)=38\), which is even but not divisible by 3.  So \(F(N)\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  If \(N\) is even and divisible by 3 but not a multiple of 6?  That's impossible because if it's divisible by 2 and 3, it's divisible by 6.  So the only numbers not divisible by 6 are either odd or even but not divisible by 3.  For such numbers, we can compute \(F(N)\) and see that it's also not divisible by 6.  Let's prove that.

**Claim.**  If \(6\nmid N\) and \(\tau(N)\ge 4\), then \(6\nmid F(N)\).

*Proof.*  Consider two cases.

- \(N\) is odd.  Then all divisors are odd, so the three smallest divisors \(d_1,d_2,d_3\) are odd.  Hence each \(N/d_i\) is odd, and their sum is odd.  Consequently \(F(N)\) is odd, thus \(6\nmid F(N)\).

- \(N\) is even but not divisible by \(3\).  Then the prime factorisation of \(N\) contains \(2\) but not \(3\).  The three smallest divisors of \(N\) are at least \(2\), and because \(3\) does not divide \(N\), the second smallest is either \(4\) (if the exponent of \(2\) is at least \(2\)) or an odd prime \(p\ge 5\) (if the exponent of \(2\) is exactly \(1\)).  In either subcase the sum of reciprocals is at most \(\frac12+\frac14+\frac15 = \frac{19}{20}<1\).  Therefore \(F(N) = N\cdot S\) with \(S<1\).  Moreover, the number \(N\) is even, and we can examine parity and divisibility by \(3\).  We need to show that \(F(N)\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  It is enough to show that \(F(N)\) is not divisible by \(3\) (or not even).  But we already know that \(N\) is not divisible by \(3\).  Since \(F(N)\) is an integer, if \(3\) divided \(F(N)\), then \(N\) would have a factor \(3\) because the fraction \(S\) is rational with denominator dividing something?  Actually let's compute modulo \(3\):

   \(N \equiv r \pmod 3\) with \(r\in\{1,2\}\).  The three smallest divisors are not multiples of \(3\).  The numbers \(N/d_i\) are integers.  We can compute \(N/d_i \pmod 3\).  Since \(d_i\) are not multiples of \(3\), each \(N/d_i \equiv r \cdot (d_i^{-1}) \pmod 3\).  So each term is \(\pm 1\) mod \(3\).  The sum of three such numbers could be \(0\) mod \(3\) if they are all equal (all \(1\) or all \(2\)).  But can that happen?  The condition for the sum to be \(0\) is that all three residues are equal.  That would require that the three divisors \(d_i\) are all congruent to the same residue mod \(3\) (since \(r\) is invertible).  This is possible (e.g., \(N=110\), \(N/d_1=55\equiv 1\), \(N/d_2=22\equiv 1\), \(N/d_3=11\equiv 2\), not all equal; but in some cases they might become all equal).  However we need a proof that \(F(N)\) is never a multiple of \(6\) when \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  The earlier example \(N=110\) gives \(F(N)=87\), which is divisible by \(3\) but not even.  So it's not a multiple of \(6\).  The claim “\(6\nmid F(N)\)” holds because \(F(N)\) is odd.  So in this case, \(F(N)\) is odd, thus not a multiple of \(6\).  So indeed, for any \(N\) not a multiple of \(6\), we have \(6\nmid F(N)\).  Let's verify the parity for \(N\) even not divisible by \(3\): we saw examples where \(F(N)\) is odd (110→87), even (40→38).  So it's not always odd.  But is it possible that \(F(N)\) becomes even and also divisible by \(3\)?  For \(N\) not a multiple of \(6\), can \(F(N)\) be a multiple of \(6\)?  That would require \(F(N)\) to be even and divisible by \(3\).  Let's test with \(N=2*5=10\) → \(F=8\) (even, not divisible by 3).  \(N=2*3^2=18\) is a multiple of 6, not considered.  \(N=2*5*7=70\) → \(F=59\) (odd).  \(N=2*5*11=110\) → \(F=87\) (odd).  \(N=2*5*13=130\) → \(F=101\) (odd).  \(N=2*5*17=170\) → \(F=136\) (even, not divisible by 3).  \(N=2*5*19=190\) → \(F=152\) (even, not divisible by 3).  So it seems that if \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(F(N)\) is never a multiple of \(6\).  This is plausible because if \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(N\) lacks either a factor \(2\) or a factor \(3\).  The transformation \(F(N)\) essentially multiplies \(N\) by a rational with denominator small, and the result often preserves the lack of a factor.  But we need a rigorous proof.

We can prove the following stronger statement:

**Lemma 2.**  If \(\tau(N)\ge 4\) and \(6\nmid N\), then \(6\nmid F(N)\).

*Proof.*  Consider the three smallest divisors \(d_1,d_2,d_3\) of \(N\) larger than \(1\).  Since \(6\nmid N\), either \(2\nmid N\) (odd) or \(3\nmid N\) (even but not multiple of 3).  

- If \(N\) is odd, then all divisors are odd, so \(d_1,d_2,d_3\) are odd.  Hence each \(N/d_i\) is odd, so their sum is odd.  Thus \(F(N)\) is odd, so \(6\nmid F(N)\).

- If \(N\) is even but \(3\nmid N\), then \(N\) is even but not divisible by \(3\).  The three smallest divisors are \(2\) and then the next smallest divisor, which could be \(4\) (if \(4\mid N\)) or an odd prime \(p\ge 5\) (if the exponent of \(2\) is exactly \(1\)).  In either case the sum of reciprocals is less than \(1\) and also the number \(F(N)\) is an integer.  Now examine modulo \(2\) and modulo \(3\).  To be a multiple of \(6\), \(F(N)\) would need to be even and divisible by \(3\).  We will show that \(F(N)\) is not divisible by \(3\).  This can be done by considering the possible residues of the divisors modulo \(3\) and using the fact that \(N\) is not divisible by \(3\).  Since \(3\nmid N\), all divisors \(d_i\) are not multiples of \(3\) (if a divisor were a multiple of \(3\), then \(N\) would be divisible by \(3\)).  Therefore \(d_i\) are invertible modulo \(3\).  Compute \(N/d_i \equiv r \cdot (d_i^{-1}) \pmod 3\), where \(r = N \pmod 3\) is \(1\) or \(2\).  So each term is \(\pm 1\) mod \(3\).  The sum of three such residues is \(0\) mod \(3\) iff they are all equal (all \(1\) or all
